    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer8545342684521953916">After a violent storm in the South Pacific in the year 1643, Roberto della Griva finds himself shipwrecked-on a ship. Swept from the Amaryllis, he has managed to pull himself aboard the Daphne, anchored in the bay of a beautiful island. The ship is fully provisioned, he discovers, but the crew is missing. <br/><br/>As Roberto explores the different cabinets in the hold, he remembers</span>
  <span id="freeText8545342684521953916" style="display:none">After a violent storm in the South Pacific in the year 1643, Roberto della Griva finds himself shipwrecked-on a ship. Swept from the Amaryllis, he has managed to pull himself aboard the Daphne, anchored in the bay of a beautiful island. The ship is fully provisioned, he discovers, but the crew is missing. <br /><br />As Roberto explores the different cabinets in the hold, he remembers chapters from his youth: Ferrante, his imaginary evil brother; the siege of Casale, that meaningless chess move in the Thirty Years' War in which he lost his father and his illusions; and the lessons given him on Reasons of State, fencing, the writing of love letters, and blasphemy.<br /><br />In this fascinating, lyrical tale, Umberto Eco tells of a young dreamer searching for love and meaning; and of a most amazing old Jesuit who, with his clocks and maps, has plumbed the secrets of longitudes, the four moons of Jupiter, and the Flood.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8545342684521953916" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_69062470" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/69062470" />
    <a title="Jon Melsæter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2673382-jon-mels-ter"><img alt="Jon Melsæter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1251401156p2/2673382.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/69062470?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jon Melsæter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jon Melsæter" href="/user/show/2673382-jon-mels-ter">Jon Melsæter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer69062470" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1103094499608017158">I can't count the times I've tried to write a review of an Eco-book, whether physically or in my head, then decided to drop it. <br/><br/>Where does one start? How does one review a product of an intellect such as Eco's, a scholar in semiotics, history and god knows what else? Many reviews I've read here on <i>The Island Of The Day Before</i> are just plain moronic - outbursts of frustration because someone expected to grasp the contexts and countless themes it covers as easily as an airport-bestseller. I have a</span>
  <span id="freeText1103094499608017158" style="display:none">I can't count the times I've tried to write a review of an Eco-book, whether physically or in my head, then decided to drop it. <br /><br />Where does one start? How does one review a product of an intellect such as Eco's, a scholar in semiotics, history and god knows what else? Many reviews I've read here on <i>The Island Of The Day Before</i> are just plain moronic - outbursts of frustration because someone expected to grasp the contexts and countless themes it covers as easily as an airport-bestseller. I have a theory that some people that like to think they know a couple of things just don't like to feel stupid, and it's true; most of Eco's books are overwhelming in their breadth and references for a reader, so much so that one ends up feeling quite stupid. <br /><br />But here's my point: Eco is firstly concerned with the polysemic and numerous ways in which <i>meaning is created and interpreted</i>, the history and epystemology of meaning, to be exact. To be able to understand the centennial intertextuality of language, symbols and meaning requires an intellect far greater than Eco or anyone else for that matter. I'm also pretty sure that Eco would facepalm himself if people assumed they could extract every meaning out his books by reading them once. Of all the authors and books out there, his are truly deserving of the cliche that the books need to be read several times to be understood. <br /><br />Eco's confidence and playfulness is what makes this book my absolute favorite. The subject, the mystery of latitude, is such a spot-on subject, and the great tapestry of references from his chosen era, the 17th century, he uses to weave this incredible story - not only in literature, but theology, astronomy, philosophy, history and science - come together in a story that is ultimately about a period of time where the paradigms of the church were cracking up, and the monopoly of truth and meaning was being heavily challenged by science. <br /><br />Eco manages to capture the mind of a young nobleman who is curious about the workings of the world and the universe, and so also the Zeitgeist of 17th century Europe: the volatility, the naivete, the wonder and the absurdity. If there ever was a point in history where the act of interpreting the world was so dynamic, it was here.<br /><br />He also channels a wide range of literary references, from Defoe to (obviously) Borges.<br /><br />In my mind, the trick to understanding how to approach Eco is like how to approach Quentin Tarantino. Tarantino makes meta-movies, i.e. films about cinema, Eco writes books about literature (and so much more!) Eco is an author's author, and with the help of his long-time collaborator and translator, William Weaver, his writing carries literary greatness in them.<br /><br />If you're just after a story, then go for something more formulaic, and steer clear.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1103094499608017158" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1530910618" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1530910618" />
    <a title="Owlseyes " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6132231-owlseyes"><img alt="Owlseyes " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594656251p2/6132231.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1530910618?book_show_action=true">Jan 30, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Owlseyes " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Owlseyes " href="/user/show/6132231-owlseyes">Owlseyes </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Owlseyes  by: </span>
      eco lei
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6132231-owlseyes?shelf=forever-on-holy-friday">forever-on-holy-friday</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6132231-owlseyes?shelf=italian-lit">italian-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6132231-owlseyes?shelf=italian">italian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6132231-owlseyes?shelf=stuck-on-friday">stuck-on-friday</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6132231-owlseyes?shelf=to-survive-you-must-tell-stories">to-survive-you-must-tell-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1530910618" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6663436120011680768">Eco:" We have a limit, a very discouraging, humiliating limit: death. That's why we like all the things that we assume have no limits and, therefore, no end. It's a way of escaping <b>thoughts about death</b>. We like lists because we don't want to die".<br/>Interview in Der Spiegel, November 11, 2009 <br/><br/> <b>UPDATE</b>; Thank you Eco...!<br/><br/>Umberto Eco, 84, Best-Selling Academic Who Navigated Two Worlds, Dies<br/>By JONATHAN KANDELLFEB. 19, 2016<br/>in: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://www.nytimes.com/2016/02/20/arts/international/umberto-eco-italian-semiotician-and-best-selling-author-dies-at-84.html'>http://www.nytimes.com/2016/02/20/art...</a><br/><br/><br/><a href='http://imgur.com/OjwWx5x' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1454165924i/17926375._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><br/>Stultus! Whom do I talk to? <br/>Misera</span>
  <span id="freeText6663436120011680768" style="display:none">Eco:" We have a limit, a very discouraging, humiliating limit: death. That's why we like all the things that we assume have no limits and, therefore, no end. It's a way of escaping <b>thoughts about death</b>. We like lists because we don't want to die".<br>Interview in Der Spiegel, November 11, 2009 <br><br> <b>UPDATE</b>; Thank you Eco...!<br><br>Umberto Eco, 84, Best-Selling Academic Who Navigated Two Worlds, Dies<br>By JONATHAN KANDELLFEB. 19, 2016<br>in: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.nytimes.com/2016/02/20/arts/international/umberto-eco-italian-semiotician-and-best-selling-author-dies-at-84.html">http://www.nytimes.com/2016/02/20/art...</a><br><br><br><a href="http://imgur.com/OjwWx5x" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1454165924i/17926375._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>Stultus! Whom do I talk to? <br>Miserable you are! What do I try?<br>I tell about my pain,<br>To the foolish seaside,<br>To the speechless stone,<br>To the deaf wind,<br>Ai, and nobody answers, <br>But the murmuring of waves. <br>[my translation]<br><br><br><a href="http://imgur.com/gZ28JRK" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1454167985i/17926719._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>(Giovan Battista Marino)<br><br><a href="http://imgur.com/uDnYOmD" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1454165924i/17926376.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a> <br><br>I just started reading it and it looks so fine.<br><br><a href="http://imgur.com/y8x6jIa" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1454165924i/17926377._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>Photo-phobic Roberto de La Grive survived the wreckage of his ship Amarilli, a fluyt (in Dutch) or, as the English said, a Flyboat.<br><br>It's year 1643. Moribund Roberto, on a piece of wood, is all alone in the ocean until he hits another Flyboat: the Daphne. He gets on board, soon to conclude the ship has been deserted. <br><br>He finds enough food, writes love letters (to "she", the "sun of his shadow"), "proud of his humiliation". Where is he? And what about that Island he envisions, though seemingly unreachable, because he doesn't know how to swim...<br><br> <blockquote>"Sun of my shadows, light of my darkness.<br>Why did Heaven not unmake me in that tempest it had so savagely provoked<br>Why save from the all devouring sea this body of mine, only to wreck my soul<br>so horribly in such mean and even more ill-starred solitude!<br>...<br>My Lady, I write you as if to offer, unworthy tribute, the withered rose of my disheartenment. And<br>yet I take pride withal in my humiliation, and as I am to this privilege condemned, almost I find joy in an abhorrent salvation; I am, I believe, alone of all our race, the only man in human memory to have been shipwrecked and cast upon a deserted ship".</blockquote><br><br>Roberto dislikes the daylight, yet, to him, the moonlight is beautiful; nights are meant to find new constellations ...<br><br>Roberto starts the exploration of the ship. Luckily, he finds water…and fruits. But he’s suspicious: there’s the sensation of the insidious; he’d heard strange sounds; plus, he’s deciphered some Latin words in the captain’s log: “quase dicitur Bubonica pestis”. The pest he had had when 13. <br><br>Now he’s got a gun, and sword and a knife. As day breaks, he seeks refuge. From the island he recognized a diversity of birds' sounds: swallows, parrots….; even crickets’. <br><br><a href="http://imgur.com/eAdiprC" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1454955887i/18034492._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a> <br><br>Roberto now remembers his life in Milano; the days at Casale; the Pozzo family; 16 years before 1640; his childhood rearing. He was a loner, having had a preceptor who taught him French. <br><br>Roberto thrived on imagination, fed by his reading of poetry and romances. <br><br> <a href="http://imgur.com/33zPNae" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1454955887i/18034493._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><blockquote>"He discovered some rough fruits that he would not have dared touch, if one of them, falling to the ground and splitting open in its ripeness, had not revealed a garnet interior. He<br>ventured to taste others, and judged them more with the tongue that speaks than with the tongue that tastes, since he defines one as a bag of honey, manna congealed in the fertility of its stem, an emerald jewel brimming with tiny rubies. Now, reading between the lines, I would venture to suggest he had discovered something very like a fig".<br>
</blockquote><br>Though priest Emanuel, back in Italy, convinced him about the Ferrante’s (his doppelgänger?) inexistence, Roberto, while on board the Daphne, cannot help conjecturing: he’s not alone, there’s someone else onboard. <br><br>Emanuel was a priest and philosopher, a follower of Democritus and Epicurus. <br><br>Roberto tries to concentrate on his survival: he’s got food for weeks, but not for months. He’s got to reach the island. Maybe there, he’ll eat of the fruit of the Tree of Oblivion; and forget about it all; and find peace. He likens Daphne to a theater of memory. <br><br>He's spotted the South Crux constellation. <br><br>In his memories, Roberto recalls the city under siege, his first love with the peasant, red-hair Ana Novarese, the one who had the courage to hold a gun and got hit; his letters to her, but also his losses: his father, his friend Saint Savin. Due to the plague ,he’ll get sick too and will lose sick Novarese. Yet he'll recover. <br><br>Mines are exploding in the besieged city; vast conjectures on the power of the machines take over. Roberto gets the advice from his master Salazar and La Saletta. When the war gets over, he returns to his village, takes care of mother till her death and a new chapter opens up in his life: Paris.<br><br>On board the Daphne Roberto dreams about the dented wheels; and finds a room full of clocks, of many types. What for? Someone must be taking care of them, because they’re working. Roberto takes a decision: to catch his malignant alter ego: the <b>Ferrante</b>; yet, due to the booze found, he looks like a fool. <br><br><a href="http://imgur.com/jPKbmdr" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1455561720i/18119153.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a> <br><a href="http://imgur.com/se4epIk" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1455561720i/18119154._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a> <br><br>Back in Paris: recollections of palaces, the nobility, his studies on “crepuscules” and “sympathy powders” eliciting healing. And the lady he meets: Lilia. But all would end, because he gets arrested under charges of conspiracy. <br><br>Cardinal Mazarino will offer Roberto a way out: he’s got an offer: a mission. Roberto accepts it. He’ll have to embark in the Dutch Amarilli ship, under the command of an English captain: Doctor Byrd.<br><br> Roberto’s mission is to know more about this secret: “the fixed point” or, in other words: the answer to the longitude problem. He’ll fake insomnia and ignorance, as well as photo-phobia; he’s now a spy for France. Even red-haired Byrd is faking, not revealing the real purpose of his travel to the Pacific Ocean. He’d been collecting flowers specimens, along the voyage. <br><br>Now there's real action, because conjecture abounds so far. Roberto has found out the intruder on board: a man, a priest, called Gaspar. The priest urges Roberto to learn to swim. Roberto tries and tries again. Gaspar tries his own way to reach the Island, inside a Campanula; and disappears. <br><br>All alone again, Roberto is back on his memories of Lilia and Ferrante. This one seems very real; he too had organized a voyage in search of the previously referred point. Ferrante wanted to subtract Lilia from Roberto. <br><br>As for conjectures, they verse on the vacuum and infinity, the plurality of worlds, the inhabitants of the moon, and the Orange Dove. Nevertheless, Roberto is not a philosopher, rather: an unhappy lover. <br><br><a href="http://imgur.com/WprB0Ba" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1457644249i/18384162._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>Roberto in his diving gets hurt by the stone-fish; it produces fever and sleep; and visits to Vessalia; a hell where God doesn’t exist. <br><br>He still thinks and dreams about his rival Ferrante.<br><br> Especially this dream gives the whole sense to the title of the book. The case is that Ferrante had a mutiny on his voyage; his body was thrown into an island, above 25 degrees of latitude. Lilia too, was thrown to the sea, “navigating” on piece of wood. But all is a nightmare of Roberto: Ferrante being killed; Ferrante facing Judas whose punishment is <i>to live forever on Holy Friday.</i><br><br>Roberto is remorse ridden for not having attained to the Island. He might have saved Lilia. <br><br>...<br><br>The last words of the book are conjectures, still. On who might have had access to Roberto’s papers? Maybe Tasman…maybe Captain Blight in 1798…, maybe….<br><br><br>This is a well accomplished book into the Philosophy of 17th/18th centuries. Philosophy of Science. <br>Worthwhile, the reading. A way into thinking and reflection. Even rocks don’t escape conjecture: how do they think? Roberto pondered on that too. <br><br><a href="http://imgur.com/E3Slp40" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1457994507i/18430061._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><br><br>For a while I've read,...under Bocage*'s eye.<br><a href="http://imgur.com/lKOSxUp" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1454165924i/17926378._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a> <br><br><br>* from Wiki:<br>Manuel Maria Barbosa du Bocage (15 September 1765 – 21 December 1805) was a Portuguese Neoclassic poet, writing at the beginning of his career under the pen name Elmano Sadino.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6663436120011680768" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18292706?book_show_action=true">Mar 21, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thomas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas" href="/user/show/954612-thomas">Thomas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18292706" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9650673980732029345">was enthralled by The Name of the Rose as a work of historical fiction; loved reading Focault's Pendulum (anyone who enjoyed reading The DaVini Code should read this to experience a real historical-religious thriller.<br/><br/>The Island of the Day Before? this book inspired me to swear never to read a book written by Umberto Eco again. why? i had not made it all the way through Chapter 1 when i encountered the following sentence:<br/><br/>"It is only later that he will assume, in dreams, that the plank, by some me</span>
  <span id="freeText9650673980732029345" style="display:none">was enthralled by The Name of the Rose as a work of historical fiction; loved reading Focault's Pendulum (anyone who enjoyed reading The DaVini Code should read this to experience a real historical-religious thriller.<br /><br />The Island of the Day Before? this book inspired me to swear never to read a book written by Umberto Eco again. why? i had not made it all the way through Chapter 1 when i encountered the following sentence:<br /><br />"It is only later that he will assume, in dreams, that the plank, by some mer­ciful decree of heaven or through the instinct of a natant object, joins in that gigue and, as it descended, naturally rises, calmed in a slow saraband-then in the choler of the elements the rules of every urbane order of dance are subverted-and with ever more elaborate periphrases it moves away from the heart of the joust, where a versipellous top spun in the hands of the sons of Aeolus, the hapless Amaryllis sinks, bowsprit aimed at the sky."<br /><br />i would not presume to consider myself the most intelligent person, but i count - in this one sentence - seven words that i needed to look up. seven!<br /><br />and it was at this point that i felt myself engaged in some silly contest with the author for the claim of Smartest Person. a contest which i can admit here, without shame, that i lost.<br /><br />all hail Umberto Eco - man able to write long sentences intelligible only with the help of a dictionary and skill at sentence-diagramming....<br /><br />...The Name of the Rose and Focault's Pendulum are still fantastic books, though...</span>
  <a data-text-id="9650673980732029345" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_164550586" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/164550586" />
    <a title="Maksym Karpovets" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5344121-maksym-karpovets"><img alt="Maksym Karpovets" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1581170883p2/5344121.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/164550586?book_show_action=true">Apr 30, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maksym Karpovets" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maksym Karpovets" href="/user/show/5344121-maksym-karpovets">Maksym Karpovets</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5344121-maksym-karpovets?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer164550586" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2692261584116605735">I have no clear idea why people don’t like this book, because I do really think that is one of the most luminous Eco’s novels. The form of <i>The Island of the Day Before</i> (1994) could seem very simple, but it is not true. As often for Eco’s literal strategy he tries to mask a various citations, allusions and parallels with cultural and historical basis. Every novel looks like intertextual garland of signs and senses which are masterly contained into historical or philosophical fiction, detective or</span>
  <span id="freeText2692261584116605735" style="display:none">I have no clear idea why people don’t like this book, because I do really think that is one of the most luminous Eco’s novels. The form of <i>The Island of the Day Before</i> (1994) could seem very simple, but it is not true. As often for Eco’s literal strategy he tries to mask a various citations, allusions and parallels with cultural and historical basis. Every novel looks like intertextual garland of signs and senses which are masterly contained into historical or philosophical fiction, detective or thriller.<br /><br />The main plot of the third novel is about Italian nobleman who is the only survivor of a shipwreck during a fierce storm. He finds himself stranded on board the Daphne, a boat anchored just offshore an unreachable island. Without wind, without crew, and without a knowing how of swimming, Roberto explores his new 'prison', having survived a shipwreck of the vessel Amaryllis.<br /><br /><i>The land made a kind of bend, edged with sand that gleamed white in the pale darkness; but, like any shipwrecked man, Roberto could not tell if it was an island or a continent</i><br /><br />Roberto tells us about his childhood, the invention of his twin Ferrante (does Eco play with archetype of twins?), his love Lilia, his initiation as a spy for Cardinal Richelieu and others. I personally like the story about the mapping the latitudes and longitudes of the planer which transforms into the philosophical reflection about borders of our world. Various details, facts and thoughts create a unique universe by Roberto’s imagination. As always, we cannot discern where reality ends and imagination starts. Otherwise, does it really matter anyway?<br /><br /><i>The smallest detail being lifelike: the reek and creak of the hull, the smell of the plants, the cries of the birds – all collaborated in forming the impression of a presence that was nothing but the effect of a phantasmagory perceived only by the mind…</i><br /><br />I agree that it is not the best Eco’s novel. But it is completely enough of great ideas and findings that make this text an excellent present of all serious readers and Eco’s fans as well.<br /><br /><b>4,5</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="2692261584116605735" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_209535198" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/209535198" />
    <a title="Patrick Neylan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1950538-patrick-neylan"><img alt="Patrick Neylan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1232826103p2/1950538.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/209535198?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick Neylan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick Neylan" href="/user/show/1950538-patrick-neylan">Patrick Neylan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1950538-patrick-neylan?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1950538-patrick-neylan?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1950538-patrick-neylan?shelf=foreign">foreign</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1950538-patrick-neylan?shelf=literary">literary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer209535198" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12333922405539445869">Readers expect Umberto Eco to take them on a stimulating journey of discovery as his characters unravel mysteries that take them to the heart of early Western civilisation. In The Name of the Rose and Foucault's Pendulum this style worked brilliantly. In the 'The Island of the Day Before' it fails catastrophically.<br/><br/>Eco spends hundreds of pages wallowing in his arcane knowledge, resorting to ever more desperate ploys to show off his learning, because this book has no plot to draw out those intelle</span>
  <span id="freeText12333922405539445869" style="display:none">Readers expect Umberto Eco to take them on a stimulating journey of discovery as his characters unravel mysteries that take them to the heart of early Western civilisation. In The Name of the Rose and Foucault's Pendulum this style worked brilliantly. In the 'The Island of the Day Before' it fails catastrophically.<br /><br />Eco spends hundreds of pages wallowing in his arcane knowledge, resorting to ever more desperate ploys to show off his learning, because this book has no plot to draw out those intellectual diversions naturally. In his previous novels, the basic murder mysteries provided a focus for the reader's journey: there was a mystery to be solved, and Eco's digressions enlightened the journey. Here the trek can be focused on one thing only: the long hoped-for last page, and the reader is only sustained by the morbid fascination of whether anything interesting is really going to happen. It doesn't.<br /><br />Very early on, our hero finds himself stuck on an abandoned ship off an uncharted island. His plight becomes a metaphor for that of the reader, trapped in Eco's ego with no hope of escape. I have a degree in Medieval Literature and History, but I can't find much of interest here. What hope is there for the more general reader? Never have I fallen asleep so often over a book, pummelled into intellectual insensibility. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12333922405539445869" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_594513598" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/594513598" />
    <a title="Vit Babenco" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco"><img alt="Vit Babenco" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365604577p2/19283284.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/594513598?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vit Babenco" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vit Babenco" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco">Vit Babenco</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer594513598" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5488200106554461681">I surmise <b>Umberto Eco</b> envisaged <i>The Island of the Day Before</i> as an antithesis of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2932.Robinson_Crusoe' title='Robinson Crusoe by Daniel Defoe' rel='nofollow noopener'>Robinson Crusoe</a> by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2007.Daniel_Defoe' title='Daniel Defoe' rel='nofollow noopener'>Daniel Defoe</a> – instead of a man surviving on a deserted island he portrayed his character secluded on a deserted ship and immersed him into all kinds of abstract cerebral musings. <br/>But this somewhat artificial idea could only have somewhat artificial realization so the novel right from the start turned into elaborate exercises in style and erudition.<br/>“Now I would say that harking back, on the ship, </span>
  <span id="freeText5488200106554461681" style="display:none">I surmise <b>Umberto Eco</b> envisaged <i>The Island of the Day Before</i> as an antithesis of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2932.Robinson_Crusoe" title="Robinson Crusoe by Daniel Defoe" rel="nofollow noopener">Robinson Crusoe</a> by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2007.Daniel_Defoe" title="Daniel Defoe" rel="nofollow noopener">Daniel Defoe</a> – instead of a man surviving on a deserted island he portrayed his character secluded on a deserted ship and immersed him into all kinds of abstract cerebral musings. <br />But this somewhat artificial idea could only have somewhat artificial realization so the novel right from the start turned into elaborate exercises in style and erudition.<br />“Now I would say that harking back, on the ship, to the Casale days helps him retrace the stages through which, as a youth, he slowly learned that the world was composed of alien architectures.<br />As if, on the one hand, finding himself now suspended between sky and sea could be only the most consequent development of his three lustra of peregrinations in a territory made up of forked paths; and, on the other hand, I believe that in reconstructing the history of his misfortunes he was seeking consolation for his present state, as if the shipwreck had restored him to that earthly paradise he had known at La Griva and had left behind on entering the walls of the besieged city.”<br />But “territory made up of forked paths” belongs to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/500.Jorge_Luis_Borges" title="Jorge Luis Borges" rel="nofollow noopener">Jorge Luis Borges</a> so <b>Umberto Eco</b> has found himself to be an anchorite in a strange land…</span>
  <a data-text-id="5488200106554461681" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_570542046" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/570542046" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/570542046?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=2019-shelf">2019-shelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=traditional-fiction">traditional-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer570542046" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer520690274615558522">A rather large part of me is astounded, yet again, at the erudition and the hopelessly convoluted tale that Umberto Eco is able to write, all when staying close to a single, simple premise. Indeed, the amount of real history, real contemporary and historical thought pre-1640's, is enough to send any regular scholar into paroxysms of joy... or the need to act on vengeance. <br/><br/>At any point the book, I can sit back and enjoy the text, the dry accounting of an anonymous scholar as he (or she) goes over</span>
  <span id="freeText520690274615558522" style="display:none">A rather large part of me is astounded, yet again, at the erudition and the hopelessly convoluted tale that Umberto Eco is able to write, all when staying close to a single, simple premise. Indeed, the amount of real history, real contemporary and historical thought pre-1640's, is enough to send any regular scholar into paroxysms of joy... or the need to act on vengeance. <br /><br />At any point the book, I can sit back and enjoy the text, the dry accounting of an anonymous scholar as he (or she) goes over the left behind documents of a shipwrecked scholar finding himself marooned on ANOTHER ship off the coast of a deserted island, unable to leave the ship because can't swim.<br /><br />Ok, a little labyrinthian. But wait! He lives and dies recounting his youth, and out of learned frustration and boredom, devises a narration of himself both fantastic and strange. A twin brother which accounts for all his crimes and failures. A life of mystery and intrigue. A lost love is given over to his fictional brother, giving him all the good things as well as the bad. <br /><br />The progression and subtle shifts throughout the novel are rather excellent.<br /><br />So why am I giving this a three star? Well, for as much as I appreciate the beautiful writing and the excellent idea behind it, it fell flat. I didn't care for either Roberto or his evil self-narrative twin. And the amount of space spent on Galenic and cutting-edge 17th-century science might be AWESOME in retrospect and conception, but a FREAKING DRAG in execution. :)<br /><br />Lordy, I can't recommend this to anyone except those who LIKE this kind of scrupulous historical drama with a HUGE dose of accurate historical erudition. This is a scholar who's trapped and a scholar who goes over this long-dead scholar's work. Ergo, it follows that the reader should ALSO be a scholar. :)<br /><br />Read this with caution. It doesn't have the charm of Baudolino or the crazy humor of Foucault's Pendulum or the awesome historical mystery of Name of the Rose. Alas. But it is nicely labyrinthian if you're into that kind of thing. :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="520690274615558522" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_85877101" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/85877101" />
    <a title="Victoria" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3179340-victoria"><img alt="Victoria" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1521355289p2/3179340.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/85877101?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Victoria" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Victoria" href="/user/show/3179340-victoria">Victoria</a>
      </span>

        rated it

<span id="freeTextContainer834108262560711954">I was recommended to read Umberto Eco by a friend of mine, and I was not disappointed at all.<br/><br/>Eco's style is a bit dense, so I can imagine it would not appeal to a lot of people.   <span id="freeText834108262560711954" style="display:none">I was recommended to read Umberto Eco by a friend of mine, and I was not disappointed at all.<br /><br />Eco's style is a bit dense, so I can imagine it would not appeal to a lot of people. However, it's also extremely lyrical and beautiful. The book itself is littered with debates on life and death, love, the nature of God and time itself. This is probably the book's greatest strength, as Eco writes so beautifully about such lofty ideals. So for anyone who's a fan of debating or philosophy would probably enjoy this book. <br /><br />I will say however, that the book itself requires a certain kind of mindset. So while I immediately loved the book and its premise, it still took me a decent amount of time to really feel as if I were absorbing it. There were times where I really wanted to read the book, but I felt way too overwhelmed at the time, or I wasn't really in the right mindset to really read it and appreciate it.<br /><br /><br /></span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1298963723?book_show_action=true">Sep 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josh" href="/user/show/6041973-josh">Josh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780151001514" href="/book/show/997996.The_Island_of_the_Day_Before">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6041973-josh?shelf=2019">2019</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1298963723" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11874622634228215171"><b>(2.5)</b><br/><br/>Eco was a great writer. His erudition was legendary and the two previous novels of his that I had completed expressed this well with great storytelling and suspense mixed with a welcome knowledge of the late Middle ages and early Modern period of Europe. While <i>'The Island of the Day Before'</i>, shares these same qualities, it is a completely different monster. At times, I found myself lost in the philosophical (perhaps pseudo-philosophical) meanderings of Roberto and his cohorts and that took </span>
  <span id="freeText11874622634228215171" style="display:none"><b>(2.5)</b><br /><br />Eco was a great writer. His erudition was legendary and the two previous novels of his that I had completed expressed this well with great storytelling and suspense mixed with a welcome knowledge of the late Middle ages and early Modern period of Europe. While <i>'The Island of the Day Before'</i>, shares these same qualities, it is a completely different monster. At times, I found myself lost in the philosophical (perhaps pseudo-philosophical) meanderings of Roberto and his cohorts and that took away from my enjoyment; I just didn't care enough to dwell deep beyond the surface of its contents. <br /><br />As I spent 3 months on this, I must say that I have NEVER stuck with a book as long as this and only did so because of my respect and enjoyment of his other works.<br /><br />There was a ton of symbolism in this that I grasped mostly, (perhaps, it went over my head at times) which was interesting, but this just wasn't a satisfying read.<br /><br />I see a mixture of 1 and 5 ratings a plenty here and they are both justifiable, depending on what you gain from this piece of Eco's oeuvre that unfortunately, at one time, left me wanting to give up not only on the book, but his works overall. <i>'Foucault's Pendulum'</i> will be my next read, but this reader isn't in a hurry for his next experience. <br /><br />Finally, if you are reading this, please do not start here. <i>'The Name of the Rose'</i> would be my first pick along with <i>'Baudolino'</i> as a second selection.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11874622634228215171" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2825701110" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2825701110" />
    <a title="J.M. Hushour" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17463213-j-m-hushour"><img alt="J.M. Hushour" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442964894p2/17463213.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2825701110?book_show_action=true">May 23, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.M. Hushour" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.M. Hushour" href="/user/show/17463213-j-m-hushour">J.M. Hushour</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2825701110" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3884496085142359509">This is a beautiful elegiac invocation of a lost age and just as good as Eco's "The Name of the Rose". However, I'd be wary of recommending this to others, because in many ways it is more of a kind of unraveling of a novel than an actual novel. Now, I don't mean that in some stupid, pedantic, pomo bullshit kind of way. Rather, it is a multi-layered ode to a certain way of thinking in a certain era of history and how they can basically cause a novel to fall to pieces once you start plucking at it</span>
  <span id="freeText3884496085142359509" style="display:none">This is a beautiful elegiac invocation of a lost age and just as good as Eco's "The Name of the Rose". However, I'd be wary of recommending this to others, because in many ways it is more of a kind of unraveling of a novel than an actual novel. Now, I don't mean that in some stupid, pedantic, pomo bullshit kind of way. Rather, it is a multi-layered ode to a certain way of thinking in a certain era of history and how they can basically cause a novel to fall to pieces once you start plucking at its beautiful, beautiful threads.<br />The plot is simple if trying: a young Italian nobleman on a mission of espionage for the Church ends up shipwrecked on a ship after his ship gets destroyed in a storm. He is stuck on the ship in the bay of a beautiful island he apparently can't get to. He has a possibly imaginary evil twin brother, a crazed series of encounters with the thought and events of the time (1640s, during the 30-Year War), and these begin to generally inform both his madness and the events unfurling around him.<br />Plot, yes, but the real beauty of the book is the way the thought of the early 17th century is woven into the characters' perceptions of what is happening around them, and here we're talking biles, humours, the Void, the science of referred pain and healing via sympathy, and so on.<br />You might, as I did, wonder what the hell was going on at any given time, but at least you are led astray beautifully!</span>
  <a data-text-id="3884496085142359509" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2358031474" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2358031474" />
    <a title="Aiden Heavilin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/65067206-aiden-heavilin"><img alt="Aiden Heavilin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515122208p2/65067206.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2358031474?book_show_action=true">May 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aiden Heavilin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aiden Heavilin" href="/user/show/65067206-aiden-heavilin">Aiden Heavilin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780151001514" href="/book/show/997996.The_Island_of_the_Day_Before">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2358031474" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5622902363159237079"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1525633683i/25491757._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Shipwrecked on a ship. Such is the predicament of Roberto della Griva, who, having survived the torments of the open ocean on board floating debris, discovers an enormous, abandoned ship resting in the water outside a mysterious island. He pulls himself on board and finds the ship empty, yet fully stocked, as if recently abandoned. The ship is vast and full of strange rooms. He discovers a garden populated with dozens of exotic birds, a room stocked with all manner of ticking clocks... As he exp</span>
  <span id="freeText5622902363159237079" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1525633683i/25491757._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Shipwrecked on a ship. Such is the predicament of Roberto della Griva, who, having survived the torments of the open ocean on board floating debris, discovers an enormous, abandoned ship resting in the water outside a mysterious island. He pulls himself on board and finds the ship empty, yet fully stocked, as if recently abandoned. The ship is vast and full of strange rooms. He discovers a garden populated with dozens of exotic birds, a room stocked with all manner of ticking clocks... As he explores the ship, he writes his account in his diary. <br><br>Later, these papers find their way to an unnamed biographer, who assembles a book about Roberto. So we see the story of Roberto through his diaries, and we see the diaries through the biographer. The story of the ship is the beating heart of the narrative, and it is orbited by an excursion through the Baroque world, as Roberto's past adventures in Europe, encountering the alchemy and science of the age. From these stories layered on stories, a twisted tale of espionage, science and magic emerges.<br><br>Such is the delicious mixture of the adventurous and arcane that rules the first half of Umberto Eco's elegant puzzlebox of a novel, <i>The Island of the Day Before</i>. I read the first one hundred and fifty pages of this book in almost one sitting. Eco's style is genial and pleasant, his characters are humorous and likable, his sense of atmosphere superb. Throughout the opening of this novel, Eco draws on a number of influences. Defoe is here, and so is Cervantes and Rabelais. Yet these antique styles are married to more modern sensibility, the influence of Borges is clear, and a bit of Pynchon has snuck in while no one was looking.<br><br><i>The Island of the Day Before</i> is a pure pleasure to read. Nearly every chapter features a new and delicious element. There are complex dialogues on mathematics, science, and theology. There are scenes of warfare and espionage. There are a few genuinely macabre villains, and a scene featuring an unfortunate dog that sent shivers down my spine. And most of all, throughout, Eco contrasts a story of survival, perhaps the most primal drive, with the highest echelons of human intellect, and finds an equilibrium between the two. Eco's book, to me, is a tale of human dignity and the wonderful drive to learn. <br><br>His style is generally restrained, but breaks sometimes into lavish lyricism. The second-to-last chapter, <i>Itinerarium Extaticum Coeleste</i> is one of the most beautiful things I have ever read, and represents a kind of confluence of all the themes Eco has developed throughout. Science, magic, storytelling, and love combine in an explosion of gorgeous, surreal writing. It has to be seen to be believed. <br><br>After reading a book as rich as <i>The Island of the Day Before</i> I am left wondering what to say in a review other than "Go and read this book immediately." I certainly haven't picked up on all the meanings and themes Eco weaves into this tale. In the face of a book of such immense learning, beauty, and joy, I am left feeling a bit unqualified, and all I can think to say is that I am going to be reading more Umberto Eco, and soon...<br><br>Strongly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5622902363159237079" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_19559572" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<span id="freeTextContainer13964794479261833646">I originally read this when it first came out, but have just completed a reread.<br/><br/>It might be described as the ultimate shaggy dog story. Eco explores language to a large extent in this book with phrases that include multiple variations on a common stem, such as it was necessary that the necessities were provided or his intention was to intend on inattention. That kind of thing anyway.<br/><br/>His protagonist is stranded on a ship somewhere near the 180th meridian and writes of his past life, loves and fan</span>
  <span id="freeText13964794479261833646" style="display:none">I originally read this when it first came out, but have just completed a reread.<br /><br />It might be described as the ultimate shaggy dog story. Eco explores language to a large extent in this book with phrases that include multiple variations on a common stem, such as it was necessary that the necessities were provided or his intention was to intend on inattention. That kind of thing anyway.<br /><br />His protagonist is stranded on a ship somewhere near the 180th meridian and writes of his past life, loves and fantasies. There's a novel within the novel too. The whole thing is ultimately unending. Just about every little trick of the novelist's art is present. Viz:<br /><br />(i) The writer purports to have discovered a manuscript, which he proceeds to interpret and transcribe to make his story, akin to Don Quixote.<br /><br />(ii) The novel within a novel. Roberto writes the story of the life of his evil twin Ferrante and his lady love Lilia, both of whose paths intertwine with Roberto's past and current life.<br /><br />(iii) Exploration of language, as mentioned already.<br /><br />Overall, this is certainly in the top five of Eco's novels (not hard as he's only published 5 to date).</span>
  <a data-text-id="13964794479261833646" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText7297056634075607898" style="display:none">I really wanted to like this book. While I'm not a big fan of Eco's books, I somehow seem to collect them, nonetheless. The premise wowed me, the cover art is righteous...and yet. And yet. The main character drove me crazy, Hamlet-style. He reminded me of the fear mongers who work 9-5 jobs, but never leave their unhappy jobs and go through life blaming others. It's like driving in the slow lane, even though all the other lanes are empty, and then getting unhappy because the slow lane is bumper-to-bumper. Do something! <br /><br />Eco is a very intelligent writer, perhaps too intelligent for moi. Try I did, but success eluded me. Instead, I felt like Tantalus, with the grapes always eluding my grasp, the water always receding. Sadness envelops me, not worthy of Umberto. Me sorry!<br /><br />Book Season = Summer (island of cerveza)</span>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/433328-judith-arvesu?shelf=literary">literary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer285129775" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6444667235465698091">Roberto della Griva abandons his sinking ship only to wash up aboard the mysteriously abandoned Dutch ship, Daphne. Within sight is the island of the day before, and if he could only swim, he could reach it, and change the direction of his fate.<br/><br/>Island of the Day Before has a deceptively simple premise, but goes way beyond it. There are actually a LOT of things going on in this book. The book not only chronicles Roberto’s days on board the Daphne, but also most of his life: from his first battle </span>
  <span id="freeText6444667235465698091" style="display:none">Roberto della Griva abandons his sinking ship only to wash up aboard the mysteriously abandoned Dutch ship, Daphne. Within sight is the island of the day before, and if he could only swim, he could reach it, and change the direction of his fate.<br /><br />Island of the Day Before has a deceptively simple premise, but goes way beyond it. There are actually a LOT of things going on in this book. The book not only chronicles Roberto’s days on board the Daphne, but also most of his life: from his first battle to his first love. It also takes us through early expeditions in the search for the prime meridian, and the competition among nations, explorers and scholars to discover or create the best, most accurate method of determining longitude.<br /><br />At first glance, Island looks like a tough, slow read. And it is. Despite this, I enjoyed the book. The elaborately poetic prose suited it, and I can see how the translator thought it necessary. Island is a highly descriptive book, and there lies its beauty. Once I got used to the language, I began to appreciate its sense of humor. It’s actually pretty funny sometimes. Roberto is such a strange creature full of contradictions: an educated nobleman with the instincts of a peasant, and somewhat of a philosopher but for the lack of common sense -- then again, he <i>is</i> a nobleman.<br /><br />It’s also interesting to see the world from the eyes of a 15th century man. The things we take for granted or as a given were rare and mysterious and impossible to understand back then. It might seem funny how they knew so little about the world — scientifically — but it’s also fascinating to see Roberto and his friends reason out the concept of time, multiple worlds, and God. Some of their ideas are patently ridiculous to us, but to them it was brilliant.<br /><br />The writing is perfectly Eco: it’s in no hurry to get anywhere, and some sentences go on forever, but the prose is finely constructed. The book is full of quotable things, my copy is so dog-eared. And the ending, while it left me hanging, suited it well. Anything else would have been anticlimatic.<br /><br />Over-all, it’s quite good though - as I said - it’s a bit of a slow read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6444667235465698091" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer820761511230562469">Bust out the champagne - I finished this book and my head didn't crack. It is one of the most amazing, yet difficult, books I've ever read. The story presents itself on multiple levels: narrative, metaphoric, historical, imaginative, etc. I'd give it five stars if it wasn't for the convoluted writing style, which made me want to give up numerous times.   <span id="freeText820761511230562469" style="display:none">Bust out the champagne - I finished this book and my head didn't crack. It is one of the most amazing, yet difficult, books I've ever read. The story presents itself on multiple levels: narrative, metaphoric, historical, imaginative, etc. I'd give it five stars if it wasn't for the convoluted writing style, which made me want to give up numerous times. I forged ahead because the question of "what's on the island?" kept nagging me.<br /><br />I loved the historical portrait of this period, when people were evolving towards a more rational world view while still tied to mythology and superstition. Some of their failed inventions and explanations sounded so logical. I had no idea that discovering how to calculate longitude was a matter of international espionage.<br /><br />If I read it again, I'd want to investigate all the historical references, which means I'd be glued to Wikipedia for the duration of the book. Most pages contain two or three references to things I don't understand, or languages I don't know. It was a daunting read but I'm glad I stuck with it.<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_9154346" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/9154346" />
    <a title="[Name Redacted]" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/287915-name-redacted"><img alt="[Name Redacted]" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1347082397p2/287915.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/9154346?book_show_action=true">Nov 15, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="[Name Redacted]" class="user" itemprop="url" name="[Name Redacted]" href="/user/show/287915-name-redacted">[Name Redacted]</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/287915-name-redacted?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/287915-name-redacted?shelf=real-world">real-world</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/287915-name-redacted?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/287915-name-redacted?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/287915-name-redacted?shelf=sciences-and-maths">sciences-and-maths</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer9154346&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer9154346" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17800207397745161379">I have been meaning to read this book since the mid-1990s when I first heard it reviewed on NPR. I finally bought a copy in the late 1990s from a used book store, but could never find the time to read it: there was always something else in the way, from other books required for my academic career to an inability to carry the sizeable hardcover around with me. It seemed I would never get through this book until I discovered that there was, in fact, an audio-book version read by none other than th</span>
  <span id="freeText17800207397745161379" style="display:none">I have been meaning to read this book since the mid-1990s when I first heard it reviewed on NPR. I finally bought a copy in the late 1990s from a used book store, but could never find the time to read it: there was always something else in the way, from other books required for my academic career to an inability to carry the sizeable hardcover around with me. It seemed I would never get through this book until I discovered that there was, in fact, an audio-book version read by none other than the great TIM CURRY! I no longer have the lengthy commute that I once had in high school, but i do have a part-time desk job (to fund my graduate education) that involves very little human interaction. No more excuses! I now have the pleasure of listening to one of my favorite voice-actors read this magnificent book. <br /><br />As for the book itself, well, it's full of the same voluptuous prose and marvelous attention to detail that we've all come to expect from Eco. The book is narrated by an anonymous scholar who recounts, based on various records, the life of a youth named Roberto shipwrecked in the South Seas in the 1600s -- though naturally there is much detail included which could not possibly have been contained in the youth's writings. Unable to swim, Roberto clings to a plank and drifts through the ocean, only to find himself marooned, not on a desert island, but on another ship which is anchored and abandoned in the bay of a desert island. He cannot leave the ship, because he cannot swim, but he cannot sail the ship alone, and so he is tormented by thoughts of his homeland, his past and the island he cannot reach...A wonderful tale and I hope to read it again (properly) in the future.<br /><br />Of particular interest is Roberto's evil older brother, a figment of his imagination which he dreamt up in his childhood and blamed for every crime of which he been wrongfully accused; as his life progresses it becomes harder to discern which deeds are in fact wrongfully attributed to Roberto and which deeds he has committed but chosen to attribute to the evil (fictional) brother. Even stranger, to occupy himself while trapped on the abandoned vessel Roberto begins writing a romance in which his imaginary brother goes on to have his own scandalous adventures and seduce the Roberto's beloved. As his writing continues, Roberto actually comes to feel the burden of guilt for the crimes committed by the imaginary brother in the fictional narrative Roberto has created, an inversion of their original relationship! Truly fascinating.<br /><br />---<br /><br />"Occasions will arise when confidence in your own perspicacity and the impulse to tell the truth will enable you to give sound advice to someone of higher station. Never do it. Every victory produces hatred in the vanquished, and if the victory is over one's own master, then it is foolish or harmful. Princes wish to be assisted, not outstripped. But you must be prudent also with your equals. Do not humiliate them by your merits. Never speak of yourself: either you will praise yourself, which is vanity, or you will denigrate yourself, which is stupidity. Rather, let others discover in you some venial sin, which envy can gnaw on without doing you too much harm. Be much but seem little. The ostrich never aspires to fly, and thus risk an exemplary fall: he allows the beauty of his feathers to be revealed gradually. And above all, if you have passions, never display them, however noble they may appear. Not everyone must be granted access to your heart. A prudent, cautious silence is the cabinet of wisdom."<br /><br />---<br /><br />"To speak always seriously provokes irritation. To be always witty, contempt. To philosophize always, sadness. To jest always, uneasiness. I have played every role, according to the time and the occasion and once in a while I have also been court jester. But this evening if you tell the story well, it will not have been a comedy but, rather, a fine tragedy."<br /><br />---<br /><br />"Lovers love their misfortunes more than their blessings."</span>
  <a data-text-id="17800207397745161379" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_240267400" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/240267400" />
    <a title="Ajk" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7057925-ajk"><img alt="Ajk" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1322424480p2/7057925.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/240267400?book_show_action=true">Nov 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ajk" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ajk" href="/user/show/7057925-ajk">Ajk</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">folks who have a thing for medieval intellectual life but not one for modern-day literature.</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7057925-ajk?shelf=funsies">funsies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7057925-ajk?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer240267400" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3777030967990421645">it was very long, and I did not enjoy it. Which is odd, honestly, because I loved Eco's Foucault's Pendulum - one of my favorite books. This one was a whole lot more like, well, slogging through 500 pages written by a Semiotics professor.<br/><br/>The plot is kind of fantastic, though. A man gets shipwrecked, latches onto a raft, and then washes up onto an abandoned ship. He can't swim, so now he's shipwrecked on a ship. this ship, of course, is riddled with secrets.<br/><br/>And then, lecturing ensues. It's all ab</span>
  <span id="freeText3777030967990421645" style="display:none">it was very long, and I did not enjoy it. Which is odd, honestly, because I loved Eco's Foucault's Pendulum - one of my favorite books. This one was a whole lot more like, well, slogging through 500 pages written by a Semiotics professor.<br /><br />The plot is kind of fantastic, though. A man gets shipwrecked, latches onto a raft, and then washes up onto an abandoned ship. He can't swim, so now he's shipwrecked on a ship. this ship, of course, is riddled with secrets.<br /><br />And then, lecturing ensues. It's all about Renaissance thought and the shaky move from Godly knowledge to secular knowledge. Again, this sounds really interesting, but somehow it wasn't executed that way. I think Eco tried to get a little too clever with his story-telling, going for the non-linear narrative, but its just not all that well executed. That said, Jules Mazarin makes an appearance, and its always nice to see him. A major plot point is the sheer inscrutability of the International Date Line and the complexity of Longitude, which is a Thing, I guess. My favorite explanation of Noah's Flood comes up in the book (the flood is full of yesterday's water coming into today), which is really neat also. Seriously, ask me some time to explain this flood theory to you, it's great stuff.<br /><br />But unlike the other Eco books I've read, this one isn't more than the sum of its parts. I just never got into it as much, and I doubt I would've been able to read the whole thing if I wasn't traveling so much. So I don't have a great verdict for this one, go ahead and skip it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3777030967990421645" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13067714978277064657">This is, by far, the most difficult novel I've ever read. I have never taken so long to finish a novel--ever. I studied the history of science and the history of early modern Europe in college (and recently refreshed my memory of both) and I'm pretty sure I caught maybe 20% of the scientific and philosophical references. Flat out brilliant. <br/><br/>As a novel, it's slow, frustrating, and unsatisfyin</span>
  <span id="freeText13067714978277064657" style="display:none">This is, by far, the most difficult novel I've ever read. I have never taken so long to finish a novel--ever. I studied the history of science and the history of early modern Europe in college (and recently refreshed my memory of both) and I'm pretty sure I caught maybe 20% of the scientific and philosophical references. At best. <br /><br />As a means of putting the reader in the mindset of an early 17th century European, it's amazing. Flat out brilliant. <br /><br />As a novel, it's slow, frustrating, and unsatisfying.<br /><br />It's kind of like going to see an art house movie when you know every character is going to die or suffer horrible afflictions, but the cast is top notch and their acting is so amazing and it's up for so many awards that you just HAVE to see it. And you're completely transported into the world...and yet at the end you feel chewed and spit out. <br /><br />This book? It's just like that. <br /><br />So, that said, I'd still recommend the novel. It's a work of art. It's just a really, really, really difficult work of art. </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3331719750917545787">Umberto Eco intellectualizes within the restraints of 17th century knowledge and superstition about the physics of the earth's rotation, natural phenomena, life, death, and love- which makes for an interesting thought-experiment, but becomes tedious for any reader living in the 21st century with more than a high-school education. The story follows a young man trapped on a 17th century ship in the lagoon of an island (Solomon Island, perhaps). Between the ship and the island supposedly runs the p</span>
  <span id="freeText3331719750917545787" style="display:none">Umberto Eco intellectualizes within the restraints of 17th century knowledge and superstition about the physics of the earth's rotation, natural phenomena, life, death, and love- which makes for an interesting thought-experiment, but becomes tedious for any reader living in the 21st century with more than a high-school education. The story follows a young man trapped on a 17th century ship in the lagoon of an island (Solomon Island, perhaps). Between the ship and the island supposedly runs the prime meridian, thus separating the two, timewise placing the island in the day before ship. In Foucault’s Pendulum, the author's explanations of the occult and macabre belief systems tied more succinctly into the novel's plot. Here, the sidebar intellectualizing of the characters as the story unfolds seems like the author's goal in and of itself, rendering the plot secondary to the accomplishment of producing intellectual debate limited to knowledge available during the 17th century. </span>

          </span>
